<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hendrick_Andriezsoon_-_Vanitas.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="595" data-file-width="460" decoding="async" height="285" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Hendrick_Andriezsoon_-_Vanitas.JPG/220px-Hendrick_Andriezsoon_-_Vanitas.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Hendrick_Andriezsoon_-_Vanitas.JPG/330px-Hendrick_Andriezsoon_-_Vanitas.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Hendrick_Andriezsoon_-_Vanitas.JPG/440px-Hendrick_Andriezsoon_-_Vanitas.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hendrick_Andriezsoon_-_Vanitas.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The human <a href="/wiki/Skull" title="Skull">skull</a> is used universally as a symbol of death</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CathedralOfTrier_Skeleton.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="726" data-file-width="335" decoding="async" height="477" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/CathedralOfTrier_Skeleton.JPG/220px-CathedralOfTrier_Skeleton.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/CathedralOfTrier_Skeleton.JPG/330px-CathedralOfTrier_Skeleton.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/84/CathedralOfTrier_Skeleton.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CathedralOfTrier_Skeleton.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of <a href="/wiki/Death_(personification)" title="Death (personification)">Death</a>, personified as a <a href="/wiki/Human_skeleton" title="Human skeleton">human skeleton</a> dressed in a <a href="/wiki/Shroud" title="Shroud">shroud</a> and clutching a <a href="/wiki/Scythe" title="Scythe">scythe</a>, from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_Trier" title="Cathedral of Trier">Cathedral of Trier</a> in <a href="/wiki/Trier" title="Trier">Trier</a>, Germany</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kuoleman_Puutarha_by_Hugo_Simberg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3695" data-file-width="4782" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Kuoleman_Puutarha_by_Hugo_Simberg.jpg/220px-Kuoleman_Puutarha_by_Hugo_Simberg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Kuoleman_Puutarha_by_Hugo_Simberg.jpg/330px-Kuoleman_Puutarha_by_Hugo_Simberg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Kuoleman_Puutarha_by_Hugo_Simberg.jpg/440px-Kuoleman_Puutarha_by_Hugo_Simberg.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kuoleman_Puutarha_by_Hugo_Simberg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The dead tending to flowers, in "The Garden of Death," <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Simberg" title="Hugo Simberg">Hugo Simberg</a> (1906)</div></div></div>
<p><b>Death</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Irreversible_process" title="Irreversible process">irreversible</a> cessation of all <a href="/wiki/Biological_process" title="Biological process">biological functions</a> that sustain an <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> It can also be defined as the irreversible cessation of functioning of the whole brain, including brainstem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Brain_death" title="Brain death">Brain death</a> is sometimes used as a legal definition of death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The remains of a former organism normally begin to <a href="/wiki/Decomposition" title="Decomposition">decompose</a> shortly after death. Death is an inevitable process that eventually occurs in <a href="/wiki/Biological_immortality" title="Biological immortality">almost all</a> organisms.
</p><p>Death is generally applied to whole organisms; the similar process seen in individual components of an organism, such as cells or tissues, is <a href="/wiki/Necrosis" title="Necrosis">necrosis</a>. Something that is not considered an organism, such as a <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">virus</a>, can be physically destroyed but is not said to die. As of the early 21st century, over 150,000 humans die each day, with <a href="/wiki/Ageing" title="Ageing">ageing</a> being by far the most common <a href="/wiki/Cause_of_death" title="Cause of death">cause of death</a>.
</p><p>Many cultures and religions have the idea of an <a href="/wiki/Afterlife" title="Afterlife">afterlife</a>, and also may hold the idea of <a href="/wiki/Judgement_(afterlife)" title="Judgement (afterlife)">judgement</a> of good and bad deeds in one's life (<a href="/wiki/Heaven" title="Heaven">heaven</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hell" title="Hell">hell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karma" title="Karma">karma</a>).
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Problems_of_definition">Problems of definition</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical_definition_of_death" title="Medical definition of death">Medical definition of death</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:StillLifeWithASkull.jpg"><img alt="Symbols of death in a painting: it shows a flower, a skull and an hourglass" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4075" data-file-width="5315" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/StillLifeWithASkull.jpg/220px-StillLifeWithASkull.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/StillLifeWithASkull.jpg/330px-StillLifeWithASkull.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/StillLifeWithASkull.jpg/440px-StillLifeWithASkull.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:StillLifeWithASkull.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A flower, a skull and an hourglass stand for <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a>, death and <a href="/wiki/Time" title="Time">time</a> in this 17th-century painting by <a href="/wiki/Philippe_de_Champaigne" title="Philippe de Champaigne">Philippe de Champaigne</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:French_-_Pendant_with_a_Monk_and_Death_-_Walters_71461.jpg"><img alt="Ivory pendant of a Monk's face. The left half of the pendant appears skeletal, while the right half appears living" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="1442" decoding="async" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/French_-_Pendant_with_a_Monk_and_Death_-_Walters_71461.jpg/220px-French_-_Pendant_with_a_Monk_and_Death_-_Walters_71461.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/French_-_Pendant_with_a_Monk_and_Death_-_Walters_71461.jpg/330px-French_-_Pendant_with_a_Monk_and_Death_-_Walters_71461.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/French_-_Pendant_with_a_Monk_and_Death_-_Walters_71461.jpg/440px-French_-_Pendant_with_a_Monk_and_Death_-_Walters_71461.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:French_-_Pendant_with_a_Monk_and_Death_-_Walters_71461.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>French â 16th-/17th-century ivory pendant, Monk and Death, recalling mortality and the certainty of death (<a href="/wiki/Walters_Art_Museum" title="Walters Art Museum">Walters Art Museum</a>)</div></div></div>
<p>The concept of death is a key to human understanding of the phenomenon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MohammadSamir_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-MohammadSamir-5">[5]</a></sup> There are many scientific approaches and various interpretations of the concept. Additionally, the advent of life-sustaining therapy and the numerous criteria for defining death from both a medical and legal standpoint, have made it difficult to create a single unifying definition.
</p><p>One of the challenges in defining death is in distinguishing it from <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a>. As a point in time, death would seem to refer to the moment at which life ends. Determining when death has occurred is difficult, as cessation of life functions is often not simultaneous across organ systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Such determination, therefore, requires drawing precise conceptual boundaries between life and death. This is difficult, due to there being little consensus on how to define life.
</p><p>It is possible to define life in terms of consciousness. When consciousness ceases, an organism can be said to have died. One of the flaws in this approach is that there are many organisms that are alive but probably not conscious (for example, single-celled organisms). Another problem is in defining consciousness, which has many different definitions given by modern scientists, psychologists and philosophers. Additionally, many religious traditions, including <a href="/wiki/Abrahamic_religions" title="Abrahamic religions">Abrahamic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dharmic_religions" title="Dharmic religions">Dharmic</a> traditions, hold that death does not (or may not) entail the end of consciousness. In certain cultures, death is more of a process than a single event. It implies a slow shift from one spiritual state to another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other definitions for death focus on the character of cessation of organismic functioning and a human death which refers to irreversible loss of personhood. More specifically, death occurs when a living entity experiences irreversible cessation of all functioning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-8">[8]</a></sup> As it pertains to human life, death is an irreversible process where someone loses their existence as a person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Historically, attempts to define the exact moment of a human's death have been subjective, or imprecise. Death was once defined as the cessation of <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heartbeat</a> (<a href="/wiki/Cardiac_arrest" title="Cardiac arrest">cardiac arrest</a>) and of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breath" title="Breath">breathing</a>, but the development of <a href="/wiki/Cardiopulmonary_resuscitation" title="Cardiopulmonary resuscitation">CPR</a> and prompt <a href="/wiki/Defibrillation" title="Defibrillation">defibrillation</a> have rendered that definition inadequate because breathing and heartbeat can sometimes be restarted. This type of death where circulatory and respiratory arrest happens is known as the circulatory definition of death (DCDD). Proponents of the DCDD believe that this definition is reasonable because a person with permanent loss of circulatory and respiratory function should be considered dead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-9">[9]</a></sup> Critics of this definition state that while cessation of these functions may be permanent, it does not mean the situation is irreversible, because if CPR was applied, the person could be revived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-9">[9]</a></sup> Thus, the arguments for and against the DCDD boil down to a matter of defining the actual words "permanent" and "irreversible," which further complicates the challenge of defining death. Furthermore, events which were <a href="/wiki/Causality" title="Causality">causally</a> linked to death in the past no longer kill in all circumstances; without a functioning heart or lungs, life can sometimes be sustained with a combination of <a href="/wiki/Life_support" title="Life support">life support</a> devices, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organ_transplants" title="Organ transplants">organ transplants</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artificial_pacemaker" title="Artificial pacemaker">artificial pacemakers</a>.
</p><p>Today, where a definition of the moment of death is required, doctors and coroners usually turn to "brain death" or "biological death" to define a person as being dead; people are considered dead when the electrical activity in their brain ceases. It is presumed that an end of electrical activity indicates the end of <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciousness</a>. Suspension of consciousness must be permanent, and not transient, as occurs during certain <a href="/wiki/Non-rapid_eye_movement_sleep" title="Non-rapid eye movement sleep">sleep</a> stages, and especially a <a href="/wiki/Coma" title="Coma">coma</a>. In the case of sleep, <a href="/wiki/Electroencephalography" title="Electroencephalography">EEGs</a> can easily tell the difference.
</p><p>The category of "brain death" is seen as problematic by some scholars. For instance, Dr. Franklin Miller, senior faculty member at the Department of Bioethics, National Institutes of Health, notes: "By the late 1990s... the equation of brain death with death of the human being was increasingly challenged by scholars, based on evidence regarding the array of biological functioning displayed by patients correctly diagnosed as having this condition who were maintained on mechanical ventilation for substantial periods of time. These patients maintained the ability to sustain circulation and respiration, control temperature, excrete wastes, heal wounds, fight infections and, most dramatically, to gestate fetuses (in the case of pregnant "brain-dead" women)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miller-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>While "brain death" is viewed as problematic by some scholars, there are certainly proponents of it that believe this definition of death is the most reasonable for distinguishing life from death. The reasoning behind the support for this definition is that brain death has a set of criteria that is reliable and reproducible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-11">[11]</a></sup> Also, the brain is crucial in determining our identity or who we are as human beings. The distinction should be made that "brain death" cannot be equated with one who is in a vegetative state or coma, in that the former situation describes a state that is beyond recovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Those people maintaining that only the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neo-cortex" title="Neo-cortex">neo-cortex</a> of the brain is necessary for consciousness sometimes argue that only electrical activity should be considered when defining death. Eventually it is possible that the criterion for death will be the permanent and irreversible loss of <a href="/wiki/Cognition" title="Cognition">cognitive</a> function, as evidenced by the death of the <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_cortex" title="Cerebral cortex">cerebral cortex</a>. All hope of recovering human thought and <a href="/wiki/Personality_psychology" title="Personality psychology">personality</a> is then gone given current and foreseeable medical technology. At present, in most places the more conservative definition of deathÂ â irreversible cessation of electrical activity in the whole brain, as opposed to just in the neo-cortexÂ â has been adopted (for example the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uniform_Determination_Of_Death_Act" title="Uniform Determination Of Death Act">Uniform Determination Of Death Act</a> in the United States). In 2005, the <a href="/wiki/Terri_Schiavo_case" title="Terri Schiavo case">Terri Schiavo case</a> brought the question of brain death and artificial sustenance to the front of <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_the_United_States" title="Politics of the United States">American politics</a>.
</p><p>Even by whole-brain criteria, the determination of brain death can be complicated. EEGs can detect spurious electrical impulses, while certain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drugs" title="Drugs">drugs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypoglycemia" title="Hypoglycemia">hypoglycemia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypoxia_(medical)" title="Hypoxia (medical)">hypoxia</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Hypothermia" title="Hypothermia">hypothermia</a> can suppress or even stop brain activity on a temporary basis. Because of this, hospitals have protocols for determining brain death involving EEGs at widely separated intervals under defined conditions.
</p><p>In the past, adoption of this whole-brain definition was a conclusion of the President's Commission for the Study of Ethical Problems in Medicine and Biomedical and Behavioral Research in 1980.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> They concluded that this approach to defining death sufficed in reaching a uniform definition nationwide. A multitude of reasons were presented to support this definition including: uniformity of standards in law for establishing death; consumption of a family's fiscal resources for artificial life support; and legal establishment for equating brain death with death in order to proceed with organ donation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aside from the issue of support of or dispute against brain death, there is another inherent problem in this categorical definition: the variability of its application in medical practice. In 1995, the American Academy of Neurology (AAN), established a set of criteria that became the medical standard for diagnosing neurologic death. At that time, three clinical features had to be satisfied in order to determine "irreversible cessation" of the total brain including: coma with clear etiology, cessation of breathing, and lack of brainstem reflexes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup> This set of criteria was then updated again most recently in 2010, but substantial discrepancies still remain across hospitals and medical specialties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>The problem of defining death is especially imperative as it pertains to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dead_donor_rule" title="Dead donor rule">dead donor rule</a>, which could be understood as one of the following interpretations of the rule: there must be an official declaration of death in a person before starting organ procurement or that organ procurement cannot result in death of the donor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-:4-9">[9]</a></sup> A great deal of controversy has surrounded the definition of death and the dead donor rule. Advocates of the rule believe the rule is legitimate in protecting organ donors while also countering against any moral or legal objection to organ procurement. Critics, on the other hand, believe that the rule does not uphold the best interests of the donors and that the rule does not effectively promote organ donation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-:4-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Signs">Signs</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Stages_of_death" title="Stages of death">Stages of death</a></div>
<p>Signs of death or strong indications that a <a href="/wiki/Endotherm" title="Endotherm">warm-blooded animal</a> is no longer alive are:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Respiratory_arrest" title="Respiratory arrest">Respiratory arrest</a> (no <a href="/wiki/Breathing" title="Breathing">breathing</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cardiac_arrest" title="Cardiac arrest">Cardiac arrest</a> (no <a href="/wiki/Heart_rate" title="Heart rate">pulse</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Brain_death" title="Brain death">Brain death</a> (no neuronal activity)</li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Postmortem_interval_changes_(stages_of_death).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1377" data-file-width="5077" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Postmortem_interval_changes_%28stages_of_death%29.png/500px-Postmortem_interval_changes_%28stages_of_death%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Postmortem_interval_changes_%28stages_of_death%29.png/750px-Postmortem_interval_changes_%28stages_of_death%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Postmortem_interval_changes_%28stages_of_death%29.png/1000px-Postmortem_interval_changes_%28stages_of_death%29.png 2x" width="500"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Postmortem_interval_changes_(stages_of_death).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Timeline of postmortem changes (stages of death).</div></div></div>
<p>The stages that follow after death are:
</p>
<ul><li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Pallor_mortis" title="Pallor mortis">Pallor mortis</a></i></span>, paleness which happens in 15â120 minutes after death</li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Algor_mortis" title="Algor mortis">Algor mortis</a></i></span>, the reduction in body temperature following death. This is generally a steady decline until matching ambient temperature</li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Rigor_mortis" title="Rigor mortis">Rigor mortis</a></i></span>, the limbs of the corpse become stiff (Latin <i>rigor</i>) and difficult to move or manipulate</li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Livor_mortis" title="Livor mortis">Livor mortis</a></i></span>, a settling of the blood in the lower (dependent) portion of the body</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Putrefaction" title="Putrefaction">Putrefaction</a>, the beginning signs of decomposition</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Decomposition" title="Decomposition">Decomposition</a>, the reduction into simpler forms of matter, accompanied by a strong, unpleasant odor.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Skeletonization" title="Skeletonization">Skeletonization</a>, the end of decomposition, where all soft tissues have decomposed, leaving only the skeleton.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">Fossilization</a>, the natural preservation of the skeletal remains formed over a very long period</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal">Legal</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Legal_death" title="Legal death">Legal death</a></div>
<p>The death of a person has legal consequences that may vary between different jurisdictions.
A <a href="/wiki/Death_certificate" title="Death certificate">death certificate</a> is issued in most jurisdictions, either by a doctor, or by an administrative office upon presentation of a doctor's declaration of death.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Misdiagnosed">Misdiagnosed</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Premature_burial" title="Premature burial">Premature burial</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiertz_burial.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1367" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Wiertz_burial.jpg/220px-Wiertz_burial.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Wiertz_burial.jpg/330px-Wiertz_burial.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Wiertz_burial.jpg/440px-Wiertz_burial.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wiertz_burial.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Antoine_Wiertz" title="Antoine Wiertz">Antoine Wiertz</a>'s painting of a man buried alive</div></div></div>
<p>There are many anecdotal references to people being declared dead by physicians and then "coming back to life", sometimes days later in their own coffin, or when <a href="/wiki/Embalming" title="Embalming">embalming</a> procedures are about to begin. From the mid-18th century onwards, there was an upsurge in the public's fear of being mistakenly buried alive,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> and much debate about the uncertainty of the signs of death. Various suggestions were made to test for signs of life before <a href="/wiki/Burial" title="Burial">burial</a>, ranging from pouring vinegar and pepper into the corpse's mouth to applying red hot pokers to the feet or into the <a href="/wiki/Rectum" title="Rectum">rectum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Writing in 1895, the physician J.C. Ouseley claimed that as many as 2,700 people were buried prematurely each year in England and Wales, although others estimated the figure to be closer to 800.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>In cases of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_shock" title="Electric shock">electric shock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cardiopulmonary_resuscitation" title="Cardiopulmonary resuscitation">cardiopulmonary resuscitation</a> (CPR) for an hour or longer can allow stunned <a href="/wiki/Nerve" title="Nerve">nerves</a> to recover, allowing an apparently dead person to survive. People found unconscious under icy water may survive if their faces are kept continuously cold until they arrive at an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emergency_room" title="Emergency room">emergency room</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Limmer_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Limmer-18">[18]</a></sup> This "diving response", in which <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolic activity</a> and oxygen requirements are minimal, is something humans share with <a href="/wiki/Cetacea" title="Cetacea">cetaceans</a> called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mammalian_diving_reflex" title="Mammalian diving reflex">mammalian diving reflex</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Limmer_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Limmer-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>As medical technologies advance, ideas about when death occurs may have to be re-evaluated in light of the ability to restore a person to vitality after longer periods of apparent death (as happened when CPR and defibrillation showed that cessation of heartbeat is inadequate as a decisive indicator of death). The lack of electrical brain activity may not be enough to consider someone scientifically dead. Therefore, the concept of information-theoretic death<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-InfoDeath_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-InfoDeath-19">[19]</a></sup> has been suggested as a better means of defining when true death occurs, though the concept has few practical applications outside the field of <a href="/wiki/Cryonics" title="Cryonics">cryonics</a>.
</p><p>There have been some scientific attempts to bring dead organisms back to life, but with limited success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> In science fiction scenarios where such technology is readily available, real death is distinguished from reversible death.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_causes_of_death_by_rate" title="List of causes of death by rate">List of causes of death by rate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Preventable_causes_of_death" title="Preventable causes of death">Preventable causes of death</a></div>
<p>The leading cause of human death in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_countries" title="Developing countries">developing countries</a> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infectious_disease" title="Infectious disease">infectious disease</a>. The leading causes in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developed_countries" title="Developed countries">developed countries</a> are <a href="/wiki/Atherosclerosis" title="Atherosclerosis">atherosclerosis</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_disease" title="Heart disease">heart disease</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">stroke</a>), <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a>, and other diseases related to <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aging" title="Aging">aging</a>. By an extremely wide margin, the largest unifying cause of death in the developed world is biological aging,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi10.2202/1941-6008.1011_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.2202/1941-6008.1011-21">[21]</a></sup> leading to various complications known as <a href="/wiki/Aging-associated_diseases" title="Aging-associated diseases">aging-associated diseases</a>. These conditions cause loss of <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">homeostasis</a>, leading to <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_arrest" title="Cardiac arrest">cardiac arrest</a>, causing loss of <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and nutrient supply, causing irreversible deterioration of the <a href="/wiki/Human_brain" title="Human brain">brain</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Tissue_(biology)" title="Tissue (biology)">tissues</a>. Of the roughly 150,000 people who die each day across the globe, about two thirds die of age-related causes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi10.2202/1941-6008.1011_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.2202/1941-6008.1011-21">[21]</a></sup> In industrialized nations, the proportion is much higher, approaching 90%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi10.2202/1941-6008.1011_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.2202/1941-6008.1011-21">[21]</a></sup> With improved medical capability, <a href="/wiki/Dying" title="Dying">dying</a> has become <a href="/wiki/Respite_care" title="Respite care">a condition to be managed</a>. Home deaths, once commonplace, are now rare in the developed world.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lewis_Hine,_Newsies_smoking_at_Skeeter%27s_Branch,_St._Louis,_1910.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3899" data-file-width="4838" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Lewis_Hine%2C_Newsies_smoking_at_Skeeter%27s_Branch%2C_St._Louis%2C_1910.jpg/220px-Lewis_Hine%2C_Newsies_smoking_at_Skeeter%27s_Branch%2C_St._Louis%2C_1910.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Lewis_Hine%2C_Newsies_smoking_at_Skeeter%27s_Branch%2C_St._Louis%2C_1910.jpg/330px-Lewis_Hine%2C_Newsies_smoking_at_Skeeter%27s_Branch%2C_St._Louis%2C_1910.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Lewis_Hine%2C_Newsies_smoking_at_Skeeter%27s_Branch%2C_St._Louis%2C_1910.jpg/440px-Lewis_Hine%2C_Newsies_smoking_at_Skeeter%27s_Branch%2C_St._Louis%2C_1910.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lewis_Hine,_Newsies_smoking_at_Skeeter%27s_Branch,_St._Louis,_1910.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>American children smoking in 1910. <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_smoking" title="Tobacco smoking">Tobacco smoking</a> caused an estimated 100 million deaths in the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-who-22">[22]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_nations" title="Developing nations">developing nations</a>, inferior sanitary conditions and lack of access to modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical_technology" title="Medical technology">medical technology</a> makes death from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infectious_diseases" title="Infectious diseases">infectious diseases</a> more common than in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developed_countries" title="Developed countries">developed countries</a>. One such disease is <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>, a bacterial disease which killed 1.8M people in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2004data_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2004data-23">[23]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">Malaria</a> causes about 400â900M cases of fever and 1â3M deaths annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AIDS" title="AIDS">AIDS</a> death toll in Africa may reach 90â100M by 2025.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Jean_Ziegler" title="Jean Ziegler">Jean Ziegler</a> (<a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> Special Reporter on the Right to Food, 2000 â Mar 2008), mortality due to <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a> accounted for 58% of the total mortality rate in 2006. Ziegler says worldwide approximately 62M people died from all causes and of those deaths more than 36M died of hunger or diseases due to deficiencies in <a href="/wiki/Micronutrient" title="Micronutrient">micronutrients</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Tobacco" title="Tobacco">Tobacco</a> smoking killed 100Â million people worldwide in the 20th century and could kill 1Â billion people around the world in the 21st century, a <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> report warned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-who-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many leading developed world causes of death can be postponed by <a href="/wiki/Diet_(nutrition)" title="Diet (nutrition)">diet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Physical_fitness" title="Physical fitness">physical activity</a>, but the accelerating incidence of disease with age still imposes limits on human <a href="/wiki/Longevity" title="Longevity">longevity</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evolution_of_aging" title="Evolution of aging">evolutionary cause of aging</a> is, at best, only just beginning to be understood. It has been suggested that direct intervention in the aging process may now be the most effective intervention against major causes of death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%C3%89douard_Manet_-_Le_Suicid%C3%A9_(ca._1877).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1445" data-file-width="1774" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/%C3%89douard_Manet_-_Le_Suicid%C3%A9_%28ca._1877%29.jpg/220px-%C3%89douard_Manet_-_Le_Suicid%C3%A9_%28ca._1877%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/%C3%89douard_Manet_-_Le_Suicid%C3%A9_%28ca._1877%29.jpg/330px-%C3%89douard_Manet_-_Le_Suicid%C3%A9_%28ca._1877%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/%C3%89douard_Manet_-_Le_Suicid%C3%A9_%28ca._1877%29.jpg/440px-%C3%89douard_Manet_-_Le_Suicid%C3%A9_%28ca._1877%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%C3%89douard_Manet_-_Le_Suicid%C3%A9_(ca._1877).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Le SuicidÃ©</i> by <a href="/wiki/%C3%89douard_Manet" title="Ãdouard Manet">Ãdouard Manet</a> depicts a man who has recently committed suicide via a firearm</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hans_Selye" title="Hans Selye">Selye</a> proposed a unified non-specific approach to many causes of death. He demonstrated that <a href="/wiki/Stress_(biology)" title="Stress (biology)">stress</a> decreases adaptability of an organism and proposed to describe the adaptability as a special resource, <i>adaptation energy</i>. The animal dies when this resource is exhausted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SelyeAE2_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-SelyeAE2-29">[29]</a></sup> Selye assumed that the adaptability is a finite supply, presented at birth. Later on, Goldstone proposed the concept of a production or income of adaptation energy which may be stored (up to a limit), as a capital reserve of adaptation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> In recent works, adaptation energy is considered as an internal coordinate on the "dominant path" in the model of adaptation. It is demonstrated that oscillations of well-being appear when the reserve of adaptability is almost exhausted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2012, <a href="/wiki/Suicide" title="Suicide">suicide</a> overtook car crashes for leading causes of human injury deaths in the U.S., followed by poisoning, falls and murder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Causes of death are different in different parts of the world. In high-income and middle income countries nearly half up to more than two thirds of all people live beyond the age of 70 and predominantly die of chronic diseases. In low-income countries, where less than one in five of all people reach the age of 70, and more than a third of all deaths are among children under 15, people predominantly die of infectious diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Autopsy">Autopsy</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_-_The_Anatomy_Lesson_of_Dr_Nicolaes_Tulp.jpg"><img alt='A painting of an autopsy, by Rembrandt, entitled "The Anatomy Lesson of Dr. Nicolaes Tulp"' class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4520" data-file-width="6000" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Rembrandt_-_The_Anatomy_Lesson_of_Dr_Nicolaes_Tulp.jpg/220px-Rembrandt_-_The_Anatomy_Lesson_of_Dr_Nicolaes_Tulp.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Rembrandt_-_The_Anatomy_Lesson_of_Dr_Nicolaes_Tulp.jpg/330px-Rembrandt_-_The_Anatomy_Lesson_of_Dr_Nicolaes_Tulp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Rembrandt_-_The_Anatomy_Lesson_of_Dr_Nicolaes_Tulp.jpg/440px-Rembrandt_-_The_Anatomy_Lesson_of_Dr_Nicolaes_Tulp.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_-_The_Anatomy_Lesson_of_Dr_Nicolaes_Tulp.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An autopsy is portrayed in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Anatomy_Lesson_of_Dr._Nicolaes_Tulp" title="The Anatomy Lesson of Dr. Nicolaes Tulp">The Anatomy Lesson of Dr. Nicolaes Tulp</a></i>, by <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt" title="Rembrandt">Rembrandt</a></div></div></div>
<p>An <a href="/wiki/Autopsy" title="Autopsy">autopsy</a>, also known as a <i>postmortem examination</i> or an <i>obduction</i>, is a <a href="/wiki/Medical_procedure" title="Medical procedure">medical procedure</a> that consists of a thorough <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical_examination" title="Medical examination">examination</a> of a human <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dead_body" title="Dead body">corpse</a> to determine the cause and manner of a person's death and to evaluate any <a href="/wiki/Disease" title="Disease">disease</a> or <a href="/wiki/Injury" title="Injury">injury</a> that may be present. It is usually performed by a specialized <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">medical doctor</a> called a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pathologist" title="Pathologist">pathologist</a>.
</p><p>Autopsies are either performed for legal or medical purposes. A forensic autopsy is carried out when the cause of death may be a criminal matter, while a clinical or academic autopsy is performed to find the medical cause of death and is used in cases of unknown or uncertain death, or for research purposes. Autopsies can be further classified into cases where external examination suffices, and those where the body is dissected and an internal examination is conducted. Permission from <a href="/wiki/Next_of_kin" title="Next of kin">next of kin</a> may be required for internal autopsy in some cases. Once an internal autopsy is complete the body is generally reconstituted by sewing it back together. Autopsy is important in a medical environment and may shed light on mistakes and help improve practices.
</p><p>A necropsy, which is not always a medical procedure, was a term previously used to describe an unregulated postmortem examination. In modern times, this term is more commonly associated with the corpses of animals.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Senescence">Senescence</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Senescence" title="Senescence">Senescence</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kameldornbaum_Sossusvlei.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="5472" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Kameldornbaum_Sossusvlei.jpg/220px-Kameldornbaum_Sossusvlei.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Kameldornbaum_Sossusvlei.jpg/330px-Kameldornbaum_Sossusvlei.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Kameldornbaum_Sossusvlei.jpg/440px-Kameldornbaum_Sossusvlei.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kameldornbaum_Sossusvlei.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dead <a href="/wiki/Vachellia_erioloba" title="Vachellia erioloba">Camelthorn tree</a> within <a href="/wiki/Sossusvlei" title="Sossusvlei">Sossusvlei</a></div></div></div>
<p>Senescence refers to a scenario when a living being is able to survive all calamities, but eventually dies due to causes relating to old age. Animal and plant cells normally reproduce and function during the whole period of natural existence, but the aging process derives from deterioration of cellular activity and ruination of regular functioning. Aptitude of cells for gradual deterioration and mortality means that cells are naturally sentenced to stable and long-term loss of living capacities, even despite continuing metabolic reactions and viability. In the United Kingdom, for example, nine out of ten of all the deaths that occur on a daily basis relates to senescence, while around the world it accounts for two-thirds of 150,000 deaths that take place daily.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed. (August 2022)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Almost all <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a> who survive external hazards to their biological functioning eventually die from <a href="/wiki/Senescence" title="Senescence">biological aging</a>, known in life sciences as "senescence". Some organisms experience <a href="/wiki/Negligible_senescence" title="Negligible senescence">negligible senescence</a>, even exhibiting <a href="/wiki/Biological_immortality" title="Biological immortality">biological immortality</a>. These include the jellyfish <i><a href="/wiki/Turritopsis_dohrnii" title="Turritopsis dohrnii">Turritopsis dohrnii</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Hydra_(genus)" title="Hydra (genus)">hydra</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Planarian" title="Planarian">planarian</a>. Unnatural causes of death include <a href="/wiki/Suicide" title="Suicide">suicide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">predation</a>. From all causes, roughly 150,000 people die around the world each day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi10.2202/1941-6008.1011_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.2202/1941-6008.1011-21">[21]</a></sup> Of these, two thirds die directly or indirectly due to senescence, but in industrialized countries â such as the United States, the United Kingdom, and Germany â the rate approaches 90% (i.e., nearly nine out of ten of all deaths are related to senescence).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi10.2202/1941-6008.1011_21-4"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.2202/1941-6008.1011-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physiological" title="Physiological">Physiological</a> death is now seen as a process, more than an event: conditions once considered indicative of death are now reversible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Where in the process a dividing line is drawn between life and death depends on factors beyond the presence or absence of <a href="/wiki/Vital_signs" title="Vital signs">vital signs</a>. In general, <a href="/wiki/Clinical_death" title="Clinical death">clinical death</a> is neither necessary nor sufficient for a determination of <a href="/wiki/Legal_death" title="Legal death">legal death</a>. A patient with working <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_heart" title="Human heart">heart</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_lung" title="Human lung">lungs</a> determined to be <a href="/wiki/Brain_death" title="Brain death">brain dead</a> can be pronounced legally dead without clinical death occurring.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cryonics">Cryonics</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cryonics" title="Cryonics">Cryonics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cryo_surgery.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="299" data-file-width="386" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Cryo_surgery.jpg/220px-Cryo_surgery.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Cryo_surgery.jpg/330px-Cryo_surgery.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/76/Cryo_surgery.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cryo_surgery.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Technicians prepare a body for cryopreservation in 1985.</div></div></div>
<p>Cryonics (from Greek ÎºÏÏÎ¿Ï 'kryos-' meaning 'icy cold') is the <a href="/wiki/Cryopreservation" title="Cryopreservation">low-temperature preservation</a> of animals and humans who cannot be sustained by contemporary medicine, with the hope that healing and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/resuscitate" title="wikt:resuscitate">resuscitation</a> may be possible in the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alcor_What_is_Cryonics_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-alcor_What_is_Cryonics-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cryopreservation" title="Cryopreservation">Cryopreservation</a> of people or large animals is not reversible with current technology. The stated rationale for cryonics is that people who are considered dead by current legal or medical definitions may not necessarily be dead according to the more stringent information-theoretic definition of death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-InfoDeath_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-InfoDeath-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whetstine_L_et_al._2005_538â542_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whetstine_L_et_al._2005_538â542-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some scientific literature is claimed to support the feasibility of cryonics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18321197_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18321197-40">[40]</a></sup> Medical science and cryobiologists generally regards cryonics with skepticism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reperfusion">Reperfusion</span></h3>
<p>"One of medicine's new frontiers: treating the dead", recognizes that cells that have been without oxygen for more than five minutes die,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> not from lack of oxygen, but rather when their <a href="/wiki/Reperfusion_injury" title="Reperfusion injury">oxygen supply is resumed</a>. Therefore, practitioners of this approach, e.g., at the Resuscitation Science institute at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania" title="University of Pennsylvania">University of Pennsylvania</a>, "aim to reduce oxygen uptake, slow metabolism and adjust the blood chemistry for gradual and safe <a href="/wiki/Reperfusion_therapy" title="Reperfusion therapy">reperfusion</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_extension">Life extension</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Life_extension" title="Life extension">Life extension</a></div>
<p>Life extension refers to an increase in <a href="/wiki/Maximum_life_span" title="Maximum life span">maximum</a> or <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">average lifespan</a>, especially in humans, by slowing down or reversing the <a href="/wiki/Senescence" title="Senescence">processes of aging</a> through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-aging" title="Anti-aging">anti-aging</a> measures. Despite the fact that aging is by far the most common cause of death worldwide, it is socially mostly ignored as such and seen as "necessary" and "inevitable" anyway, which is why little money is spent on research into anti-aging therapies, a phenomenon known as the <a href="/wiki/Pro-aging_trance" title="Pro-aging trance">pro-aging trance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi10.2202/1941-6008.1011_21-5"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.2202/1941-6008.1011-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Average lifespan is determined by vulnerability to <a href="/wiki/Accident" title="Accident">accidents</a> and age or lifestyle-related afflictions such as <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">cardiovascular disease</a>. Extension of average lifespan can be achieved by good <a href="/wiki/Diet_(nutrition)" title="Diet (nutrition)">diet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Exercise" title="Exercise">exercise</a> and avoidance of hazards such as <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_smoking" title="Tobacco smoking">smoking</a>. Maximum lifespan is also determined by the rate of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aging" title="Aging">aging</a> for a species inherent in its <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a>. Currently, the only widely recognized method of extending maximum lifespan is <a href="/wiki/Calorie_restriction" title="Calorie restriction">calorie restriction</a>. Theoretically, extension of maximum lifespan can be achieved by reducing the rate of aging damage, by <a href="/wiki/Tissue_engineering" title="Tissue engineering">periodic replacement of damaged tissues</a>, or by <a href="/wiki/Nanobiotechnology" title="Nanobiotechnology">molecular repair</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rejuvenation_(aging)" title="Rejuvenation (aging)">rejuvenation</a> of deteriorated cells and tissues.
</p><p>A United States poll found that religious people and irreligious people, as well as men and women and people of different economic classes have similar rates of support for life extension, while Africans and Hispanics have higher rates of support than white people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> 38 percent of the polled said they would desire to have their aging process cured.
</p><p>Researchers of life extension are a subclass of biogerontologists known as "biomedical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gerontologist" title="Gerontologist">gerontologists</a>". They try to understand the nature of aging and they develop treatments to reverse aging processes or to at least slow them down, for the improvement of health and the maintenance of youthful vigor at every stage of life. Those who take advantage of life extension findings and seek to apply them upon themselves are called "life extensionists" or "longevists". The primary life extension strategy currently is to apply available anti-aging methods in the hope of living long enough to benefit from a complete cure to aging once it is developed.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Location">Location</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hugo_Sundstr%C3%B6m_-_Kallio_with_Mannerheim.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1286" data-file-width="2126" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Hugo_Sundstr%C3%B6m_-_Kallio_with_Mannerheim.jpg/220px-Hugo_Sundstr%C3%B6m_-_Kallio_with_Mannerheim.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Hugo_Sundstr%C3%B6m_-_Kallio_with_Mannerheim.jpg/330px-Hugo_Sundstr%C3%B6m_-_Kallio_with_Mannerheim.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Hugo_Sundstr%C3%B6m_-_Kallio_with_Mannerheim.jpg/440px-Hugo_Sundstr%C3%B6m_-_Kallio_with_Mannerheim.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hugo_Sundstr%C3%B6m_-_Kallio_with_Mannerheim.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ky%C3%B6sti_Kallio" title="KyÃ¶sti Kallio">KyÃ¶sti Kallio</a> (in the middle), the fourth <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/President_of_the_Republic_of_Finland" title="President of the Republic of Finland">President of the Republic of Finland</a>, had a fatal heart attack a few seconds after this photograph was taken by Hugo SundstrÃ¶m on December 19, 1940 at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Helsinki_railway_station" title="Helsinki railway station">Helsinki railway station</a> in <a href="/wiki/Helsinki" title="Helsinki">Helsinki</a>, Finland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Before about 1930, most people in Western countries died in their own homes, surrounded by family, and comforted by clergy, neighbors, and doctors making <a href="/wiki/House_call" title="House call">house calls</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-8018-1762-5_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-8018-1762-5-47">[47]</a></sup> By the mid-20th century, half of all Americans died in a hospital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-679-41461-4_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-679-41461-4-48">[48]</a></sup> By the start of the 21st century, only about 20â25% of people in developed countries died outside of a medical institution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-679-41461-4_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-679-41461-4-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16299059_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16299059-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11535743_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11535743-50">[50]</a></sup> The shift away from dying at home towards dying in a professional medical environment has been termed the "Invisible Death".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-679-41461-4_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-679-41461-4-48">[48]</a></sup> This shift occurred gradually over the years, until most deaths now occur outside the home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychology">Psychology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Death_anxiety" title="Death anxiety">Death anxiety</a></div>
<p>Death studies is a field within <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxforddeathpsych_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-oxforddeathpsych-52">[52]</a></sup> Many people are afraid of <a href="/wiki/Dying" title="Dying">dying</a>. Discussing, thinking about, or planning for their own deaths causes them discomfort. This fear may cause them to put off financial planning, preparing a <a href="/wiki/Will_and_testament" title="Will and testament">will and testament</a>, or requesting help from a <a href="/wiki/Hospice" title="Hospice">hospice</a> organization.
</p><p>Different people have different responses to the idea of their own deaths. Philosopher <a href="/wiki/Galen_Strawson" title="Galen Strawson">Galen Strawson</a> writes that the death that many people wish for is an instant, painless, unexperienced annihilation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-53">[53]</a></sup> In this unlikely scenario, the person dies without realizing it and without being able to fear it. One moment the person is walking, eating, or sleeping, and the next moment, the person is dead. Strawson reasons that this type of death would not take anything away from the person, as he believes that a person cannot have a legitimate claim to ownership in the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Death_and_culture" title="Death and culture">Death and culture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Human_skull_symbolism" title="Human skull symbolism">Human skull symbolism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hertig_Karl_skymfande_Klaus_Flemings_lik,_m%C3%A5lning_av_Albert_Edelfelt_fr%C3%A5n_1878.jpg"><img alt="A duke insulting the corpse of Klaus Fleming" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="915" data-file-width="1080" decoding="async" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Hertig_Karl_skymfande_Klaus_Flemings_lik%2C_m%C3%A5lning_av_Albert_Edelfelt_fr%C3%A5n_1878.jpg/220px-Hertig_Karl_skymfande_Klaus_Flemings_lik%2C_m%C3%A5lning_av_Albert_Edelfelt_fr%C3%A5n_1878.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Hertig_Karl_skymfande_Klaus_Flemings_lik%2C_m%C3%A5lning_av_Albert_Edelfelt_fr%C3%A5n_1878.jpg/330px-Hertig_Karl_skymfande_Klaus_Flemings_lik%2C_m%C3%A5lning_av_Albert_Edelfelt_fr%C3%A5n_1878.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Hertig_Karl_skymfande_Klaus_Flemings_lik%2C_m%C3%A5lning_av_Albert_Edelfelt_fr%C3%A5n_1878.jpg/440px-Hertig_Karl_skymfande_Klaus_Flemings_lik%2C_m%C3%A5lning_av_Albert_Edelfelt_fr%C3%A5n_1878.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hertig_Karl_skymfande_Klaus_Flemings_lik,_m%C3%A5lning_av_Albert_Edelfelt_fr%C3%A5n_1878.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The regent duke Charles (later king <a href="/wiki/Charles_IX_of_Sweden" title="Charles IX of Sweden">Charles IX of Sweden</a>) insulting the corpse of <a href="/wiki/Klaus_Fleming" title="Klaus Fleming">Klaus Fleming</a>. <a href="/wiki/Albert_Edelfelt" title="Albert Edelfelt">Albert Edelfelt</a>, 1878</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Placid_death.JPG"><img alt="A naturally mummified body (from Guanajuato)" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="2667" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Placid_death.JPG/220px-Placid_death.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Placid_death.JPG/330px-Placid_death.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Placid_death.JPG/440px-Placid_death.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Placid_death.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dead bodies can be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mummified" title="Mummified">mummified</a> either naturally, as this one <a href="/wiki/Mummies_of_Guanajuato" title="Mummies of Guanajuato">from Guanajuato</a>, or by intention, as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_burial_customs" title="Ancient Egyptian burial customs">those in ancient Egypt</a></div></div></div>
<p>In society, the nature of death and humanity's <a href="/wiki/Mortality_salience" title="Mortality salience">awareness of its own mortality</a> has for millennia been a concern of the world's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_traditions" title="Religious traditions">religious traditions</a> and of <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophical inquiry</a>. This includes belief in <a href="/wiki/Resurrection" title="Resurrection">resurrection</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Afterlife" title="Afterlife">afterlife</a> (associated with <a href="/wiki/Abrahamic_religions" title="Abrahamic religions">Abrahamic religions</a>), <a href="/wiki/Reincarnation" title="Reincarnation">reincarnation</a> or rebirth (associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dharmic_religions" title="Dharmic religions">Dharmic religions</a>), or that consciousness permanently ceases to exist, known as <a href="/wiki/Eternal_oblivion" title="Eternal oblivion">eternal oblivion</a> (associated with <a href="/wiki/Secular_humanism" title="Secular humanism">Secular humanism</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>Commemoration ceremonies after death may include various <a href="/wiki/Mourning" title="Mourning">mourning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Funeral" title="Funeral">funeral</a> practices and ceremonies of honouring the deceased. The physical remains of a person, commonly known as a <i>corpse</i> or <i>body</i>, are usually <a href="/wiki/Burial" title="Burial">interred</a> whole or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cremated" title="Cremated">cremated</a>, though among the world's cultures there are a variety of other methods of <a href="/wiki/Disposal_of_human_corpses" title="Disposal of human corpses">mortuary disposal</a>. In the English language, blessings directed towards a dead person include <i><a href="/wiki/Rest_in_peace" title="Rest in peace">rest in peace</a></i> (originally the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>requiescat in pace</i>), or its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Initialism" title="Initialism">initialism</a> RIP.
</p><p>Death is the center of many traditions and organizations; customs relating to death are a feature of every culture around the world. Much of this revolves around the care of the dead, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Afterlife" title="Afterlife">afterlife</a> and the disposal of bodies upon the onset of death. The <a href="/wiki/Disposal_of_human_corpses" title="Disposal of human corpses">disposal of human corpses</a> does, in general, begin with the <a href="/wiki/Last_offices" title="Last offices">last offices</a> before significant time has passed, and ritualistic ceremonies often occur, most commonly interment or <a href="/wiki/Cremation" title="Cremation">cremation</a>. This is not a unified practice; in <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a>, for instance, the body is given a <a href="/wiki/Sky_burial" title="Sky burial">sky burial</a> and left on a mountain top. Proper preparation for death and techniques and ceremonies for producing the ability to transfer one's spiritual attainments into another body (<a href="/wiki/Reincarnation" title="Reincarnation">reincarnation</a>) are subjects of detailed study in Tibet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mummification" title="Mummification">Mummification</a> or <a href="/wiki/Embalming" title="Embalming">embalming</a> is also prevalent in some cultures, to retard the rate of <a href="/wiki/Decomposition" title="Decomposition">decay</a>.
</p><p>Legal aspects of death are also part of many cultures, particularly the settlement of the deceased <a href="/wiki/Estate_(law)" title="Estate (law)">estate</a> and the issues of <a href="/wiki/Inheritance" title="Inheritance">inheritance</a> and in some countries, <a href="/wiki/Inheritance_tax" title="Inheritance tax">inheritance taxation</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Grave_stone2.JPG"><img alt="Gravestones in Japan" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2818" data-file-width="2003" decoding="async" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Grave_stone2.JPG/170px-Grave_stone2.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Grave_stone2.JPG/255px-Grave_stone2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Grave_stone2.JPG/340px-Grave_stone2.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Grave_stone2.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gravestones in <a href="/wiki/Kyoto" title="Kyoto">Kyoto</a>, Japan</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Capital_punishment" title="Capital punishment">Capital punishment</a> is also a culturally divisive aspect of death. In most jurisdictions where capital punishment is carried out today, the death penalty is reserved for premeditated <a href="/wiki/Murder" title="Murder">murder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Espionage" title="Espionage">espionage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Treason" title="Treason">treason</a>, or as part of <a href="/wiki/Military_justice" title="Military justice">military justice</a>. In some countries, sexual crimes, such as <a href="/wiki/Adultery" title="Adultery">adultery</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sodomy" title="Sodomy">sodomy</a>, carry the death penalty, as do religious crimes such as <a href="/wiki/Apostasy" title="Apostasy">apostasy</a>, the formal renunciation of one's religion. In many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Use_of_capital_punishment_by_nation" title="Use of capital punishment by nation">retentionist</a> countries, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drug_trafficking" title="Drug trafficking">drug trafficking</a> is also a capital offense. In China, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trafficking_in_human_beings" title="Trafficking in human beings">human trafficking</a> and serious cases of <a href="/wiki/Political_corruption" title="Political corruption">corruption</a> are also punished by the death penalty. In militaries around the world <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Courts-martial" title="Courts-martial">courts-martial</a> have imposed death sentences for offenses such as <a href="/wiki/Cowardice" title="Cowardice">cowardice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Desertion" title="Desertion">desertion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Insubordination" title="Insubordination">insubordination</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mutiny" title="Mutiny">mutiny</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Death in warfare and in <a href="/wiki/Suicide_attack" title="Suicide attack">suicide attack</a> also have cultural links, and the ideas of <i><a href="/wiki/Dulce_et_decorum_est_pro_patria_mori" title="Dulce et decorum est pro patria mori">dulce et decorum est pro patria mori</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Mutiny" title="Mutiny">mutiny</a> punishable by death, grieving relatives of dead soldiers and <a href="/wiki/Death_notification" title="Death notification">death notification</a> are embedded in many cultures. Recently in the western world, with the increase in terrorism following the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a>, but also further back in time with suicide bombings, <a href="/wiki/Kamikaze" title="Kamikaze">kamikaze</a> missions in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> and suicide missions in a host of other conflicts in history, death for a cause by way of suicide attack, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martyrdom" title="Martyrdom">martyrdom</a> have had significant cultural impacts.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allisvanity.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1219" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="335" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Allisvanity.jpg/220px-Allisvanity.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Allisvanity.jpg/330px-Allisvanity.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Allisvanity.jpg/440px-Allisvanity.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Allisvanity.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>All is Vanity</i> by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Allan_Gilbert" title="Charles Allan Gilbert">Charles Allan Gilbert</a> is an example of a <i><a href="/wiki/Memento_mori" title="Memento mori">memento mori</a></i>, intended to represent how life and death are intertwined</div></div></div>
<p>Suicide in general, and particularly <a href="/wiki/Euthanasia" title="Euthanasia">euthanasia</a>, are also points of cultural debate. Both acts are understood very differently in different cultures. In Japan, for example, ending a life with honor by <a href="/wiki/Seppuku" title="Seppuku">seppuku</a> was considered a desirable death, whereas according to traditional Christian and Islamic cultures, suicide is viewed as a sin. <a href="/wiki/Death_(personification)" title="Death (personification)">Death is personified</a> in many cultures, with such symbolic representations as the <a href="/wiki/Death_(personification)" title="Death (personification)">Grim Reaper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Azrael" title="Azrael">Azrael</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> god <a href="/wiki/Yama" title="Yama">Yama</a> and <a href="/wiki/Father_Time" title="Father Time">Father Time</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Muerte-Blanca_6.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="580" data-file-width="427" decoding="async" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Muerte-Blanca_6.jpg/220px-Muerte-Blanca_6.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Muerte-Blanca_6.jpg/330px-Muerte-Blanca_6.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/45/Muerte-Blanca_6.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Muerte-Blanca_6.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Santa_Muerte" title="Santa Muerte">Santa Muerte</a>, the personification of death in Mexican tradition<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In Brazil, a human death is counted officially when it is registered by existing family members at a cartÃ³rio, a government-authorized registry. Before being able to file for an official death, the deceased must have been registered for an official birth at the cartÃ³rio. Though a Public Registry Law guarantees all Brazilian citizens the right to register deaths, regardless of their financial means, of their family members (often children), the Brazilian government has not taken away the burden, the hidden costs and fees, of filing for a death. For many impoverished families, the indirect costs and burden of filing for a death lead to a more appealing, unofficial, local, cultural burial, which in turn raises the debate about inaccurate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mortality_rates" title="Mortality rates">mortality rates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Talking about death and witnessing it is a difficult issue with most cultures. Western societies may like to treat the dead with the utmost material respect, with an official embalmer and associated rites. Eastern societies (like India) may be more open to accepting it as a <i>fait accompli</i>, with a funeral procession of the dead body ending in an open-air burning-to-ashes of the same.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Consciousness">Consciousness</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Consciousness_after_death" title="Consciousness after death">Consciousness after death</a></div>
<p>Much interest and debate surround the question of what happens to one's consciousness as one's body dies. The belief in the permanent loss of consciousness after death is often called <i><a href="/wiki/Eternal_oblivion" title="Eternal oblivion">eternal oblivion</a></i>. Belief that the <a href="/wiki/Stream_of_consciousness_(psychology)" title="Stream of consciousness (psychology)">stream of consciousness</a> is preserved after physical death is described by the term <i><a href="/wiki/Afterlife" title="Afterlife">afterlife</a></i>. Neither are likely to ever be confirmed without the ponderer having to actually die.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_biology">In biology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Death&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">September 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Earthworm.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1067" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Earthworm.jpg/250px-Earthworm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Earthworm.jpg/375px-Earthworm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Earthworm.jpg/500px-Earthworm.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Earthworm.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Earthworm" title="Earthworm">Earthworms</a> are soil-dwelling detritivores</div></div></div>
<p>After death, the remains of a former organism become part of the <a href="/wiki/Biogeochemical_cycle" title="Biogeochemical cycle">biogeochemical cycle</a>, during which animals may be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Necrophagy" title="Necrophagy">consumed</a> by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predator" title="Predator">predator</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Scavenger" title="Scavenger">scavenger</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_material" title="Organic material">Organic material</a> may then be further decomposed by <a href="/wiki/Detritivore" title="Detritivore">detritivores</a>, organisms which recycle <a href="/wiki/Detritus" title="Detritus">detritus</a>, returning it to the environment for reuse in the <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food chain</a>, where these chemicals may eventually end up being consumed and assimilated into the cells of an organism. Examples of detritivores include <a href="/wiki/Earthworm" title="Earthworm">earthworms</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Woodlice" title="Woodlice">woodlice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dung_beetle" title="Dung beetle">dung beetles</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">Microorganisms</a> also play a vital role, raising the temperature of the decomposing matter as they break it down into yet simpler molecules. Not all materials need to be fully decomposed. <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">Coal</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> formed over vast tracts of time in <a href="/wiki/Swamp" title="Swamp">swamp</a> ecosystems, is one example.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_selection">Natural selection</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Competition_(biology)" title="Competition (biology)">Competition (biology)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">Natural selection</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">Extinction</a></div>
