<p>Contemporary <a href="/wiki/History_of_evolutionary_thought" title="History of evolutionary thought">evolutionary theory</a> sees death as an important part of the process of <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> It is considered that organisms less <a href="/wiki/Adaptation" title="Adaptation">adapted</a> to their environment are more likely to die having produced fewer offspring, thereby reducing their contribution to the <a href="/wiki/Gene_pool" title="Gene pool">gene pool</a>. Their genes are thus eventually bred out of a population, leading at worst to <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinction</a> and, more positively, making the process possible, referred to as <a href="/wiki/Speciation" title="Speciation">speciation</a>. Frequency of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_reproduction" title="Biological reproduction">reproduction</a> plays an equally important role in determining species survival: an organism that dies young but leaves numerous offspring displays, according to <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Darwinian</a> criteria, much greater <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darwinian_fitness" title="Darwinian fitness">fitness</a> than a long-lived organism leaving only one.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extinction">Extinction</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">Extinction</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ExtinctDodoBird.jpeg"><img alt="Painting of a dodo" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="244" data-file-width="225" decoding="async" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/ExtinctDodoBird.jpeg/170px-ExtinctDodoBird.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e0/ExtinctDodoBird.jpeg 1.5x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ExtinctDodoBird.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Dodo" title="Dodo">dodo</a>, the bird that became a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byword_(saying)" title="Byword (saying)">byword</a> in the English language for the extinction of a species<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Diamond_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Diamond-62">[62]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Extinction is the cessation of existence of a species or group of <a href="/wiki/Taxon" title="Taxon">taxa</a>, reducing <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>. The moment of extinction is generally considered to be the death of the last individual of that species (although the <a href="/wiki/Population_bottleneck" title="Population bottleneck">capacity to breed and recover</a> may have been lost before this point). Because a species' potential <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Range_(biology)" title="Range (biology)">range</a> may be very large, determining this moment is difficult, and is usually done retrospectively. This difficulty leads to phenomena such as <a href="/wiki/Lazarus_taxon" title="Lazarus taxon">Lazarus taxa</a>, where species presumed extinct abruptly "reappear" (typically in the <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> record) after a period of apparent absence. New species arise through the process of <a href="/wiki/Speciation" title="Speciation">speciation</a>, an aspect of <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a>. New varieties of organisms arise and thrive when they are able to find and exploit an <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">ecological niche</a> â and species become extinct when they are no longer able to survive in changing conditions or against superior competition.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution_of_aging_and_mortality">Evolution of aging and mortality</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_ageing" title="Evolution of ageing">Evolution of ageing</a></div>
<p>Inquiry into the evolution of aging aims to explain why so many living things and the vast majority of animals weaken and die with age (exceptions include <i><a href="/wiki/Hydra_(genus)" title="Hydra (genus)">Hydra</a></i> and the jellyfish <i><a href="/wiki/Turritopsis_dohrnii" title="Turritopsis dohrnii">Turritopsis dohrnii</a></i>, which research shows to be <a href="/wiki/Biological_immortality" title="Biological immortality">biologically immortal</a>). The evolutionary origin of <a href="/wiki/Senescence" title="Senescence">senescence</a> remains one of the fundamental puzzles of biology. <a href="/wiki/Gerontology" title="Gerontology">Gerontology</a> specializes in the science of human aging processes.
</p><p>Organisms showing only <a href="/wiki/Asexual_reproduction" title="Asexual reproduction">asexual reproduction</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>, some <a href="/wiki/Protist" title="Protist">protists</a>, like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euglenoid" title="Euglenoid">euglenoids</a> and many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amoebozoan" title="Amoebozoan">amoebozoans</a>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unicellular" title="Unicellular">unicellular</a> organisms with <a href="/wiki/Sexual_reproduction" title="Sexual reproduction">sexual reproduction</a> (<a href="/wiki/Colony_(biology)" title="Colony (biology)">colonial</a> or not, like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volvocales" title="Volvocales">volvocine</a> algae <i><a href="/wiki/Pandorina" title="Pandorina">Pandorina</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Chlamydomonas" title="Chlamydomonas">Chlamydomonas</a></i>) are "immortal" at some extent, dying only due to external hazards, like being eaten or meeting with a fatal accident. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multicellular" title="Multicellular">multicellular</a> organisms (and also in <a href="/wiki/Multinucleate" title="Multinucleate">multinucleate</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ciliates" title="Ciliates">ciliates</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> with a <a href="/wiki/Weismann_barrier" title="Weismann barrier">Weismannist development</a>, that is, with a division of labor between mortal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Somatic_cells" title="Somatic cells">somatic (body) cells</a> and "immortal" <a href="/wiki/Germ_cell" title="Germ cell">germ (reproductive) cells</a>, death becomes an essential part of life, at least for the somatic line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gilbert_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gilbert-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Volvox" title="Volvox">Volvox</a></i> algae are among the simplest organisms to exhibit that division of labor between two completely different cell types, and as a consequence include death of somatic line as a regular, genetically regulated part of its <a href="/wiki/Biological_life_cycle" title="Biological life cycle">life history</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gilbert_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gilbert-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_views">Religious views</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Buddhism">Buddhism</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Anussati#The_ten_recollections" title="Anussati">Anussati Â§Â The ten recollections</a></div>
<p>In Buddhist doctrine and practice, death plays an important role. Awareness of death was what motivated <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Prince Siddhartha</a> to strive to find the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amrta#Theravada_Buddhism" title="Amrta">"deathless"</a> and finally to attain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enlightenment_(Buddhism)" title="Enlightenment (Buddhism)">enlightenment</a>. In Buddhist doctrine, death functions as a reminder of the value of <a href="/wiki/Human_beings_in_Buddhism" title="Human beings in Buddhism">having been born as a human being</a>. Being reborn as a human being is considered the only state in which one can attain enlightenment. Therefore, death helps remind oneself that one should not take life for granted. The belief in rebirth among Buddhists does not necessarily remove <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Existential_angst" title="Existential angst">death anxiety</a>, since all existence in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samsara_(Buddhism)" title="Samsara (Buddhism)">cycle of rebirth</a> is considered filled with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dukkha" title="Dukkha">suffering</a>, and being reborn many times does not necessarily mean that one progresses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blum_2004_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blum_2004-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>Death is part of several key Buddhist tenets, such as the <a href="/wiki/Four_Noble_Truths" title="Four Noble Truths">Four Noble Truths</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prat%C4%ABtyasamutp%C4%81da" title="PratÄ«tyasamutpÄda">dependent origination</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blum_2004_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blum_2004-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Christianity">Christianity</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Soul_in_the_Bible" title="Soul in the Bible">Soul in the Bible</a>, <a href="/wiki/Second_death" title="Second death">Second death</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_the_dead#Christianity" title="Resurrection of the dead">Resurrection of the dead Â§Â Christianity</a></div><p>While there are different sects of Christianity with different branches of belief; the overarching ideology on death grows from the knowledge of afterlife. Meaning after death the individual will undergo a separation from mortality to immortality; their soul leaves the body entering a realm of spirits. Following this separation of body and spirit (i.e. death) <a href="/wiki/Resurrection" title="Resurrection">resurrection</a> will occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Representing the same transformation <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus Christ</a> embodied after his body was placed in the tomb for three days. Like <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Him</a>, each person's body will be resurrected reuniting the spirit and body in a perfect form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> This process allows the individuals soul to withstand death and transform into life after death.
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hinduism">Hinduism</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Reincarnation#Hinduism" title="Reincarnation">Reincarnation Â§Â Hinduism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naraka_(Hinduism)" title="Naraka (Hinduism)">Naraka (Hinduism)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yama_(Hinduism)" title="Yama (Hinduism)">Yama (Hinduism)</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Death&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">August 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Reincarnation_AS.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="592" data-file-width="409" decoding="async" height="318" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Reincarnation_AS.jpg/220px-Reincarnation_AS.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Reincarnation_AS.jpg/330px-Reincarnation_AS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f3/Reincarnation_AS.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Reincarnation_AS.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration depicting Hindu beliefs about <a href="/wiki/Reincarnation" title="Reincarnation">reincarnation</a></div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Hindu_texts" title="Hindu texts">Hindu texts</a>, death is described as the individual eternal spiritual <i><a href="/wiki/Jiva" title="Jiva">jiva-atma</a></i> (soul or conscious self) exiting the current temporary material body. The soul exits this body when the body can no longer sustain the conscious self (life), which may be due to mental or physical reasons, or more accurately, the inability to act on one's <i><a href="/wiki/Kama#In_Hinduism" title="Kama">kama</a></i> (material desires). During conception, the soul enters a compatible new body based on the remaining merits and demerits of one's <i><a href="/wiki/Karma#In_Hinduism" title="Karma">karma</a></i> (good/bad material activities based on <i><a href="/wiki/Dharma#Hinduism" title="Dharma">dharma</a></i>) and the state of one's mind (impressions or last thoughts) at the time of death.
</p><p>Usually the process of <a href="/wiki/Reincarnation#Hinduism" title="Reincarnation">reincarnation</a> (soul's transmigration) makes one forget all memories of one's previous life. Because nothing really dies and the temporary material body is always changing, both in this life and the next, death simply means forgetfulness of one's previous experiences (previous material identity).
</p><p>Material existence is described as being full of miseries arising from birth, disease, old age, death, mind, weather, etc. To conquer <i><a href="/wiki/Sa%E1%B9%83s%C4%81ra" title="Saá¹sÄra">samsara</a></i> (the cycle of death and rebirth) and become eligible for one of the different types of <i><a href="/wiki/Moksha" title="Moksha">moksha</a></i> (liberation), one has to first conquer <i><a href="/wiki/Kama#In_Hinduism" title="Kama">kama</a></i> (material desires) and become <a href="/wiki/Enlightenment_(spiritual)" title="Enlightenment (spiritual)">self-realized</a>. The human form of life is most suitable for this spiritual journey, especially with the help of <i><a href="/wiki/Sadhu" title="Sadhu">sadhu</a></i> (self-realized saintly persons), <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sastra" title="Sastra">sastra</a></i> (revealed spiritual scriptures), and <i><a href="/wiki/Guru" title="Guru">guru</a></i> (self-realized spiritual masters), given all three are in agreement.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islam">Islam</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_view_of_death" title="Islamic view of death">Islamic view of death</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judaism">Judaism</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yahrtzeit_candle.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Yahrtzeit_candle.JPG/220px-Yahrtzeit_candle.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Yahrtzeit_candle.JPG/330px-Yahrtzeit_candle.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Yahrtzeit_candle.JPG/440px-Yahrtzeit_candle.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yahrtzeit_candle.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Yahrzeit_candle" title="Yahrzeit candle">yahrzeit candle</a> lit in memory of a loved one on the anniversary of the death</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Bereavement_in_Judaism" title="Bereavement in Judaism">Bereavement in Judaism</a></div>
<p>There are a <a href="/wiki/Jewish_eschatology#The_world_to_come" title="Jewish eschatology">variety of beliefs about the afterlife</a> within Judaism, but none of them contradict the preference of life over death. This is partially because death puts a cessation to the possibility of fulfilling any <a href="/wiki/Mitzvah" title="Mitzvah">commandments</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leonardo_Skeleton_1511.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="594" data-file-width="475" decoding="async" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Leonardo_Skeleton_1511.jpg/220px-Leonardo_Skeleton_1511.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Leonardo_Skeleton_1511.jpg/330px-Leonardo_Skeleton_1511.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Leonardo_Skeleton_1511.jpg/440px-Leonardo_Skeleton_1511.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leonardo_Skeleton_1511.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Study of Skeletons</i>, c. 1510, by <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a></div></div></div>
<p>The word "death" comes from <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> <i>dÄaÃ¾</i>, which in turn comes from Proto-Germanic *<i>dauÃ¾uz</i> (reconstructed by etymological analysis). This comes from the Proto-Indo-European stem *<i>dheu-</i> meaning the "process, act, condition of <a href="/wiki/Dying" title="Dying">dying</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>The concept and symptoms of death, and varying degrees of delicacy used in discussion in public forums, have generated numerous scientific, legal, and socially acceptable terms or euphemisms. When a person has died, it is also said they have "passed away", "passed on", "expired", or "gone", among other socially accepted, religiously specific, slang, and irreverent terms.
</p><p>As a formal reference to a dead person, it has become common practice to use the <a href="/wiki/Participle" title="Participle">participle</a> form of "decease", as in "the deceased"; another noun form is "<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/decedent" title="wikt:decedent">decedent</a>".
</p><p>Bereft of life, the dead person is a "corpse", "<a href="/wiki/Cadaver" title="Cadaver">cadaver</a>", "body", "set of remains" or, when all flesh is gone, a "<a href="/wiki/Skeleton" title="Skeleton">skeleton</a>". The terms "<a href="/wiki/Carrion" title="Carrion">carrion</a>" and "carcass" are also used, usually for dead non-human animals. The ashes left after a <a href="/wiki/Cremation" title="Cremation">cremation</a> are lately called "cremains".
</p>

<li id="cite_note-InfoDeath-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-InfoDeath_19-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-InfoDeath_19-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFMerkle">Merkle, Ralph. <a class="external text" href="http://www.merkle.com/definitions/infodeath.html" rel="nofollow">"Information-Theoretic Death"</a>. <i>merkle.com</i>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20160809190714/http://www.merkle.com/definitions/infodeath.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 9 August 2016<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">4 June</span> 2016</span>. <q>A person is dead according to the information-theoretic criterion if the structures that encode memory and personality have been so disrupted that it is no longer possible in principle to recover them. If inference of the state of memory and personality are feasible in principle, and therefore restoration to an appropriate functional state is likewise feasible in principle, then the person is not dead.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=merkle.com&amp;rft.atitle=Information-Theoretic+Death&amp;rft.aulast=Merkle&amp;rft.aufirst=Ralph&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.merkle.com%2Fdefinitions%2Finfodeath.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADeath"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation news cs1" id="CITEREFMcKie2002">McKie, Robin (13 July 2002). <a class="external text" href="https://www.theguardian.com/education/2002/jul/14/medicalscience.science" rel="nofollow">"Cold facts about cryonics"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20170708232125/https://www.theguardian.com/education/2002/jul/14/medicalscience.science" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 8 July 2017<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">1 December</span> 2013</span>. <q>Cryonics, which began in the Fifties, is the freezing â usually in liquid nitrogen â of human beings who have been legally declared dead. The aim of this process is to keep such individuals in a state of refrigerated limbo so that it may become possible in the future to resuscitate them, cure them of the condition that killed them, and then restore them to functioning life in an era when medical science has triumphed over the activities of the Banana Reaper</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Guardian&amp;rft.atitle=Cold+facts+about+cryonics&amp;rft.date=2002-07-13&amp;rft.aulast=McKie&amp;rft.aufirst=Robin&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.theguardian.com%2Feducation%2F2002%2Fjul%2F14%2Fmedicalscience.science&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADeath"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-alcor_What_is_Cryonics-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-alcor_What_is_Cryonics_38-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="http://alcor.org/AboutCryonics/index.html" rel="nofollow">"What is Cryonics?"</a>. <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcor_Foundation" title="Alcor Foundation">Alcor Foundation</a></i>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20131203002857/http://alcor.org/AboutCryonics/index.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 3 December 2013<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2 December</span> 2013</span>. <q>Cryonics is an effort to save lives by using temperatures so cold that a person beyond help by today's medicine might be preserved for decades or centuries until a future medical technology can restore that person to full health.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Alcor+Foundation&amp;rft.atitle=What+is+Cryonics%3F&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Falcor.org%2FAboutCryonics%2Findex.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADeath"></span></span>
